<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="duress(n)">&#182; Dures.</form>
<xpln lang="en">DUres is where one 
is kept in pryson, 
</page>
 
<page sig="74r">
or restrained from his 
liberty contrary to the 
order of the lawe, and 
if such a person so bee&#173;

especialty or obligati&#173;
ion by reason of such 
imprisonment, such a 
deede is voide in the 
lawe: and in an action 
brought vpon such an

that it was made by 
dures of imprisonm<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t, 
but if a man bee arres&#173;
ted vppon any action, 
at the suit of an other, 
thoughe the cause of 
the action be not good 
nor trewe, if he make 
any obligatyon to a 
straunger beeinge in 
prison by such arrest, 
yet it shall not be said 
by dures, but if hee 
make an obligatyon 
to him at whose suite 
he was arrested to bee 
discharged of such im&#173;
prisonment, th<expan type="e+_">en</expan> it shal 
be said dures.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="eyre(n)" lexeme="justice(n)">&#182; Eire Iustices.</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="embracer(n)">&#182; Embrasour or Embra&#173;
ceour</form>
<xpln lang="en">EMbrasour or Em&#173;

and speaketh in the

or ouerloke th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>, there&#173;
by to put th<expan type="e+_">em</expan> in feare 
and doubt of the mat&#173;

may speake in the case, 
</page>
 
<page sig="75r">
for their fee, but they 
may not labor the Iu&#173;
rye, and if they take

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="encroachment(n)">&#182; Encrochment.</form>
<xpln lang="en">ENcrochment is said 
when the Lord hath

his tenaunt, then of 
ryght is due, or ought 
to bee payed or done 
vnto him: As if the 
tenaunt holde his land 
of his Lorde by feal&#173;
tie and ij.s.rent yere&#173;
lye, And nowe of late 
tyme, the Lorde hath 
gotten seisin of iij. shil&#173;
inges rent, or of ho&#173;
mage or Escuage, or 
such like, Then thys 
is called an Encroch&#173;
ment of that rent or 
seruice.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="inheritance(n)">&#182; Enheritance.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="inheritance(n)" lexeme="corporate(a)" lexeme="incorporate(a)">ENheritance is such

</page>
 
<page sig="75v">
tenementes, or other 
things, as may be in&#173;
herited by the heire, 
whether it be of estate 
in fee simple, or tayle, 
by dyscent from a&#173;
ny of hys Auncesters, 
or by hys owne pur&#173;
chase. 
 
And Enheritaunce 
is deuyded into two 
sortes, that is to say, 
enheritance corporate, 
and enherit<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ce incorpo<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
rate. 
 
Enheritaunce cor&#173;
porate are mesuages, 
lands, meadowes, pas&#173;
tures, rentes, and such 
like that haue sub&#173;
stance in them selues, 
and may haue conty&#173;
nuance alwaies. And 
these are called corpo&#173;
rall thinges 
 
Enheritance incor&#173;
porate, are aduow&#173;
sons, villaines, waies, 
commons, Courtes, 
fishinges, and such 
like that are, or may be 
appendant, or appurte&#173;
</page>
 
<page sig="76r">
naunt to inheritances 
corporate.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="equity(n)">&#182; Equitie.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EQuitie is in two 
sorz, differing much 
the one from the other, 
&#38; are of contrary effec&#173;
tes, for the one doth a&#173;
bridge, diminish and 
take from the letter of 
the lawe. The other 
doth inlarge, amplifie 
&#38; adde thereunto. 
 
The first is thus de&#173;
fined, Equitie is the 
correction of a law ge&#173;
nerally made, in that 
part wherin it faileth, 
which correction of the 
generall wordes, is 
much vsed in our law. 
As if for example, 
when an act of parli&#173;
ament is made, that 
whosoeuer doth such 
a thing, shalbe a felon, 
&#38; shal suffer death: yet 
if a madde man, or an 
infant of yonge yeres 
that hath no dyscreti&#173;
on, doe the same, they 
</page>
 
<page sig="76v">
shalbe no felons, nor 
suffer death therefore. 
 
Also if a statute 
were made that al per&#173;
sons that shal receiue, 
or giue meate or drink 
or other succour, to 
any that shal doe such 
a thinge, shal be acces&#173;
sory to his offence, &#38; 
shal suffer death if they 
did knowe of the fact, 
yet notwithstandinge 
one doth such an act, 
and commeth to his 
wife, who knowinge 
thereof doth receiue 
him and giues hym 
meat and drinke, shee 
shal not be accessorye 
nor felon, for in the 
generaltye of the said 
wordes of the lawe, 
he that is mad, nor <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
infant, nor the wife 
were included in mea&#173;
ninge. And thus equi&#173;
ty doth correct the ge&#173;
neralty of the law in 
those cases, &#38; the ge&#173;
neral wordes are by 
equity abridged. 
</page>
 
<page sig="77r">
The other equitye 
is defined after thys 
sorte: Equity is wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan>
the words of the law 
are effectually directed 
and one thinge onely 
prouided by <expan type="y+e">the</expan> wordes 
of the lawe, to the end 
that al thinges of like 
kinde may be prouided 
by the same. And so 
when the wordes e&#173;
nact one thinge, they 
enact al other thinges 
that are of like degree. 
As the statute which 
ordeines that in an ac&#173;
tyon of debt against 
executors, he <expan type="y+t">that</expan> doth 
appere by distresse shal 
aunswere, doth extend 
by equity to adminis&#173;
trators, for he of them 
that doth first appeare 
by distresse, shal aun&#173;
swere by equytye of 
the sayd acte, Bee&#173;
cause they are of like 
kinde. 
 
So likewise <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sta&#173;
tute of Glocest. geues 
the actyon of wast, and 
</page>
 
<page sig="77v">
the paine thereof, a&#173;
gainst him that holds 
for life, or yeares, and 
by the equyty of the 
same a man shall haue 
an actyon of wast a&#173;
gainst him that hol&#173;
deth but for one yeare, 
or halfe yeare, and yet 
that is without the 
wordes of the statute, 
for he that holdeth but 
for halfe a yeare, or 
one yere, doth not hold 
for yeres, but that is 
the meaninge, and the 
words that enact the 
one by equitye, enacte 
the other.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="escape(n)">&#182; Escape.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="voluntary(a)" lexeme="negligent(a)">EScape is in two 
sortes, that is 
to say, volunta&#173;
ry, and neglygent. 
 
Voluntary escape, 
is wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan> one doth arrest 
an other for felony, or 
other crime, &#38; after let<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
teth him go where he 
wil, this lett<expan type="i+_">in</expan>g of him 
</page>
 
<page sig="78r">
to goe is a voluntarie 
escape. And if the ar&#173;
rest of him that esca&#173;
ped were for felonye, 
then that shal be felo&#173;
ny in him that did suf<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
fer <expan type="y+e">the</expan> escape, and if for 
treason, then it shalbe 
treason in him, and if 
for trespas, then tres&#173;
pas, &#38; so of other. 
 
Negligent escape, 
is when one is arres&#173;
ted and after escapes 
against the wil of him 
that did so arrest him. 
and is not freshly pur&#173;
sued and taken before 
the pursuer looseth <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
sight of him, this shal 
be said a negligent es&#173;
cape, not withstanding 
that hee out of whose 
possession he escaped, 
doe take him after hee 
left sight of him. 
 
There is an escape 
also without arrest, 
as if a murder be made 
in the daye, and the 
murderer be not tak<expan type="e+_">en</expan>, 
then it is an escape, 
</page>
 
<page sig="78v">
for the which <expan type="y+e">the</expan> towne 
where <expan type="y+e">the</expan> murder was 
done shalbe amerced.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="esplees(n)">&#182; Esplees.</form>
<xpln lang="en">ESplees is as it 
were the seysin, or 
possession of a thinge 
profit, or commodity 
that is to bee taken. 
As of a common the 
esplees is the takinge 
of the grasse or c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>mon 
by the mouthes of the 
beastes that common 
there: Of an aduows<expan type="o+_">on</expan>
the takinge of grosse 
tithes: Of a wood, the 
selling of wood, of an 
orchard the selling of 
Aples or other fruit 
growinge there: of a 
mill the taking of toll 
is the esplees, and of 
such like.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="essoin(n)">&#182; Essoine.</form>
<xpln lang="en">ESsoine is where an 
action is brought, &#38; 
the plaintife or defen&#173;
</page>
 
<page sig="79r">
dant may not wel ap&#173;
peare at the day in 
court, for one of the 
v. causes vnder expres&#173;
sed, then hee shal bee 
essoined to saue his de&#173;
faut, whereupon note 
well that there bee v. 
maner of essoines, that 
is to say, essoyne de 
ouster le mere, and that 
is by xl. daies, the se&#173;
cond essoine is <term lang="la">de terra 
sancta</term>, and that shalbe 
by a yere &#38; a day, and 
these two shalbe layed 
in the beginning of the 
plee. The iii. essoine is 
<term lang="la">de male vener</term>, and that 
shalbe at c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>mon daies, 
as the action requireth 
and this is called the 
common essoine. The 
iiij. essoine is <term lang="la">de malo 
lecti</term>, &#38; that is onely in 
a writte of right and 
there vpon there shall 
a writte go out of the 
Chauncerie, directed 
to the sherife, that hee 
shall send iiii. knights 
to the tenant to see the 
</page>
 
<page sig="79v">
tenaunt, and if he bee 
sicke, to geeue him a 
day after a yere and a 
day. The v. essoyne is 
<term lang="fr">de seruice le roy</term>, and it 
lyeth in al actions, ex&#173;
cept in assise of nouel 
disseisin, writ of dower 
darreine presentment, 
and in appell of mur&#173;
der: but in thys es&#173;
soine, it behoueth at 
the day to shewe hys 
warrant, or els it shal 
torne into a defaut, if 
it be in a plee real, or he 
shal loose xx.s. for the 
plaintifes iorney, or 
more by the discretion 
of the Iustices, if it be 
in plee personall, as it 
appeareth by the sta&#173;
tute of Gloucester. 
cap. 8.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="estoppel(n)">&#182; Estoppell.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EStoppell is when 
one is concluded, &#38; 
forbidden in lawe to 
speake agaynst hys 
owne acte, or deede. 
</page>
 
<page sig="80r">
yea, although it bee to 
say the truth: And of 
estoppells there are a 
great many, one for ex&#173;
ample is, when I. S. 
is bound in an obliga&#173;
tion by the name of 
Thomas Stile, or a&#173;
ny other name, and is 
afterward sued accor&#173;
ding to the same name 
put in the obligation, 
that is to saye Tho&#173;
mas Stile, Nowe hee 
shal not be receiued to 
say that hee is misna&#173;
med, but shalbe driuen 
to aunswere accor&#173;
dinge to the name put 
in the obligation, that 
is to saye T. S. for 
peraduenture the ob&#173;
ligee did not know his 
name, but by the re&#173;
port onely of the obli&#173;
gor himselfe: and in 
as much, as he is the 
same man that was 
bound: he shalbe estop&#173;
ped and forbidden in 
law to say the contra&#173;
rie against his owne 
</page>
 
<page sig="80v">
deede: for otherwyse 
hee myght take ad&#173;
uantage of hys owne 
wronge, which the 
lawe will not suffer a 
man to doe. 
 
Also if the daughter 
that is onely heire to 
her father, wil sue ly&#173;
uery with her Sister 
that is a bastarde, shee 
shal not afterward bee 
receiued to say that her 
Sister is bastarde, in 
so much that yf her 
bastarde Sister take 
halfe the lande wyth 
her, there is no remedy 
by law. 
 
Also a man seised 
of landes in fee simple, 
will take a lease for 
yeres of the same land 
of a straunger by deede 
indented, this is an 
estoppell duringe the 
terme of yeares. And 
the lessee is thereby 
barred to saye the 
trouth, for the trouth 
is, that he that lessed 
the land had nothing 
</page>
 
<page sig="81r">
in it at the time of the 
lease made, and that 
the fee simple was in 
him that did take the 
lease. But this he shal 
not be receyued to say, 
till after the yeres are 
determined, because it 
appeareth that he hath 
an estate for yeares, &#38; 
it was hys folly to 
take a leas of his owne 
landes, and therefore 
shall thus be punished 
for his folly.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="estranger(n)">&#182; Estraungers.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EStraungers are 
they, that are not 
parties nor priuies to 
the leuying of a fine, 
or making of a deede.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="estray(n)">&#182; Estray.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EStray, is where 
any beast or cattel 
is in any Lordshippe, 
and none knoweth the 
owner thereof, then it 
shalbe seised to the vse 
</page>
 
<page sig="81v">
of the Queene, or of 
the Lorde that hath 
suche Estray by the 
Queenes graunt, or 
by prescription, and if 
the owner come and 
make clayme thereto, 
<expan type="w+t">with</expan>in a yere &#38; a day, th<expan type="e+_">en</expan>
he shal haue it agayne 
paying for his meat, 
or els after <expan type="y+e">the</expan> yere, the 
propertie therof shalbe 
to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Lord, so <expan type="y+t">that</expan> the lord 
make <expan type="p_,">pro</expan>clamation ther 
of according to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> law, 
in ij. Market townes.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="excommunication(n)">&#182; Excommunication.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EXcommunication, 
is when a man by 
iudgement in the spy&#173;
ritual Court is accur&#173;
sed, then his is disabled 
to sue any acti<expan type="o+_">on</expan> in the 
Queenes court, and if 
he remaine excommu&#173;
nicate xl. dayes, and 
will not bee iustified 
by hys Ordinarye, 
then the Byshoppe 
shall send hys letter 
</page>
 
<page sig="82r">
patent to the Chaun&#173;
cellor, and thereupon 
it shalbe commaunded 
to the Sherife to take 
the bodie of him that 
is accursed, by a writ 
called <term lang="la">de Excommuni&#173;
cato capiendo</term>, till hee 
hath made agreement 
with the Church for 
the contempte and 
wronge, and when he 
is iustified and hath 
made agreement, then 
the Byshoppe shall 
sende his letters to the 
Queene, certifiynge 
the same, and then it 
shalbe commaunded to 
the Shirife to dely&#173;
uer hym, by a writte 
called <term lang="la">Excommunicato 
deliberando</term>.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="exchange(n)">&#182; Exchange.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EXchange, is where 
a man is seised of 
certeine lande, and an 
other man is seysed 
of other lande, if they 
by a deede indented, 
</page>
 
<page sig="82v">
or without dede (if <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
landes be in one selfe 
county) exh<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ge their 
landes, so that euerye 
of them shal haue o&#173;
thers landes to hym, 
so exchanged in fee, fee 
taile, or for terme of 
lyfe, that is called an 
exchange, and it is 
good with out lyuerie 
and seisine. And in ex&#173;
change it beehooueth 
that the estats to th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
limited by <expan type="y+e">the</expan> exchang be 
equal, for if one haue 
an estate in fee in hys 
l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>d, &#38; the other hath 
estate in <expan type="y+e">the</expan> other lande 
but for terme of life, 
or in taile, th<expan type="e+_">en</expan> such es&#173;
change is void, but if 
the estates bee equall, 
and the landes bee not 
of equall value, yet 
the exchange is good. 
Also an exchange of 
rent for landes is good 
so an exchaunge bee&#173;
twene rent and com&#173;
mon is good and that 
ought to bee by dede. 
</page>
 
<page sig="83r">
And it behooueth al&#173;
way that these words 
(exchange) be in the 
dede, or els nothinge 
passeth by the deede, 
except that he haue 
lyuerie and seisine.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="execution(n)">&#182; Execution.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EXecution is where 
iudgement is gee&#173;
uen in any actyon 
that the plaintife shal 
recouer the lande, the 
debt, or damages, as <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
case is, and when the 
writt is awarded to 
put him in possession, 
that is called a writte 
of Execution, &#38; wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan>
he hath the possession 
of the land, or is pay&#173;
ed of the debt, or dam&#173;
mages, or hath the bo&#173;
dy of the defendaunt 
awarded to prison, th<expan type="e+_">en</expan>
hee hath execution; 
and if the plee bee in 
the County, or court 
baron, or hundred, and 
they deferre <expan type="y+e">the</expan> iudge&#173;
</page>
 
<page sig="83v">
ment in fauour of the 
partie, or for other 
cause, then the dem<expan type="a+_">an</expan>&#173;
dant shall haue a writ 
of <term lang="la">Executione iudicij</term>. 
But in a writ of debt 
a man shall not haue 
recouerie of any land, 
but of that which the 
defend<expan type="a+_">an</expan>t hath the day 
of the iudgement yel&#173;
ded. And of chattells 
a man shall haue exe&#173;
cution onelye of the 
chatels which he hath 
the day of execution 
sued.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="executor(n)">&#182; Executour.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EXecutor, is when a 
man maketh hys 
testament and last wil, 
and therin nameth the 
person that shall exe&#173;
cute his testam<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t, then 
he that is so named is 
hys executour, &#38; such 
an executor shal haue 
an action against eue&#173;
rie debtour of his tes&#173;
tatour, and if the exe&#173;
</page>
 
<page sig="84r">
cutours haue assetts, 
euery one to whom 
the testatour was in&#173;
debt, shall haue an 
action against the exe&#173;
cutour, if hee haue an 
oblygation or specy&#173;
altie, but in euerye 
case where the testa&#173;
tour myght wage his 
lawe, no action ly&#173;
eth agaynst the exe&#173;
cutour.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="extinguishment(n)">&#182; Extinguishment.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EXtinguishment, is 
where a Lorde of a 
manor, or any other, 
hath a rent going out 
of lande, and he pur&#173;
chaseth the same land, 
so that hee hath such 
estate in the lande as 
hee hath in the rent, 
then the rent is ex&#173;
tinct, for that, that a 
manne may not haue 
rent going out of his 
owne land. And when 
any rent shal bee ex&#173;
tinct, it behoueth that 
</page>
 
<page sig="84v">
the lande and the rent 
be in one hande, and al 
so that the estate that 
he hath be not defeasi&#173;
ble, and that he haue 
as good estate in the 
land as in the rent, for 
if he haue estate in the 
lande but for terme of 
life or yeares, &#38; hath 
a fee simple in the rent, 
then the rent ys not 
extinct, but is in sus&#173;
pence for that time, &#38; 
then after <expan type="y+e">the</expan> terme the 
rent is reuiued. Also if 
there be Lord, Mesne, 
&#38; tenaunt, &#38; the Lord 
purchaseth the tenan&#173;
cie, then the mesnalty 
is extinct, but that 
mesne shal haue <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sur&#173;
plusage of the rent, if 
there be any, as a rent 
secke. Also if a man 
haue a hygh way ap&#173;
pendant, &#38; after pur&#173;
chase the land wherein 
the hygh way is, then 
the way is extinct, &#38; so 
it is of a common ap&#173;
pendant.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>
 
<page sig="85r">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="extortion(n)">&#182; Extorcion.</form>
<xpln lang="en">EXtorcion is a wrong 
done by an Officer, 
as a Maior, Baylife, 
Sherife, Eschetor, or 
other officer, by colour 
of his office, in taking 
excessiue reward or fee, 
for execution of hys 
saide office or other&#173;
wise, and is no other&#173;
wise, and is no other 
thinge in deede then 
plaine robberie, or ra&#173;
ther more odious then 
robberie, for robberie 
is apparant, and al&#173;
wayes hath with it 
the counten<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ce of vice, 
but extorcion being as 
great a vice as robbe&#173;
rie is, carrieth with it 
a countenance of ver&#173;
tue, by meanes wher&#173;
of, it is the more hard 
to be tryed, or discer&#173;
ned, and therefore the 
more odyous, and yet 
some there be that wil 
not sticke to stretch 
their office, credyte, 
and conscyence, to 
</page>
 
<page sig="85v">
purchase money as wel 
by extortion as other&#173;
wise, according to the 
sayinge of the Poet 
Virgil. 
 
What can be told? or 
what is <expan type="y+t">that</expan> that hunger 
sweete of golde doth 
not constrayne men 
mortal to attempt?</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="failing(n)" lexeme="record(n)">&#182; Failing of Record.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FAiling of Recorde, 
is when an action 
of trespas or such like 
is brought against one 
and the defendant say&#173;
eth, that the plaintife 
before thys brought 
an action for the same 
trespasse in an other 
court, &#38; recouered d<expan type="a+_">am</expan>&#173;
mage &#38; c. And dema<expan type="u+_">un</expan>&#173;
deth iudgement of the 
court, if he shal againe 
haue this action &#38;c. &#38; 
the plaintif saith there 
is no such record, wher 
vpon the defendaunt 
hath a day gyuen him 
to bring in the record, 
</page>
 
<page sig="86r">
at which day he fay&#173;
leth, or bringeth in 
suche a one, as is no 
barre to this action, 
then he is said to faile 
of his recorde, &#38; there 
vpon the plaintife shal 
haue iudgement to re&#173;
couer &#38;c.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="deed(n)">&#182; Deede.</form>
<xpln lang="en">DEede is a proofe 
and testimonie of 
the agreement of the 
partie whose deede it 
is, to the thinge con&#173;
tayned in the deede, as 
a deede of feoffement 
is a proofe of the ly&#173;
uerie of seisin: for the 
lande passeth by the li&#173;
uerie of seisine, but 
when the deede and the 
lyuerie are ioyned to&#173;
gether, that is a proofe 
of the liuerye, and 
that the feoffour is 
contented, that the 
feoffee shall haue the 
lande. 
 
And note that all 
</page>
 
<page sig="86v">
deedes are either ind<expan type="e+_">en</expan>&#173;
ted, whereof there bee 
two, three, or more as 
the case requireth, of 
whiche the feoffour, 
gra<expan type="u+_">un</expan>tor, or lessor hath 
one, the feffee, gra<expan type="u+_">un</expan>tee, 
or lessee an other, and 
peraduenture some o&#173;
ther bodie also ano&#173;
ther &#38;c. or els they are 
poll deedes or single, &#38; 
but one, which the fef&#173;
fee, grauntee, or lessee 
hath &#38;c. 
 
And euery deede con&#173;
sisteth of iij. principall 
points, (and if those 
iij. be not ioyned toge&#173;
ther, it is no perfect 
deede to binde the par&#173;
ties) namely, writing, 
sealing, and deliuery. 
 
The first point is 
writing, whereby is 
shewed the partyes 
names to the deede, 
their dwelling places, 
their degrees, the thing 
graunted, vpon what 
c<expan type="o+_">on</expan>siderations, thestate 
limitted, <expan type="y+e">the</expan> time when 
</page>
 
<page sig="87r">
it was graunted, and 
whether simply or vp&#173;
pon condicion, wyth 
other such like circum<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
stances. But whether 
the parties vnto the 
deede, writ in the ende 
their owne names, or 
set therto their marks 
(as it is commonlye 
vsed,) it maketh no 
matter at all (as I 
thinke) for that is not 
ment where it is said, 
that euery deede ought 
to haue writing. 
 
The second poynt is 
sealinge, which is a 
farther testimonie of 
their consents to that 
contayned in the deede 
as it appereth by these 
wordes. In witnesse 
whereof &#38;c. alwayes 
put in that later end of 
deedes, without which 
woordes the deede is 
insufficient. 
 
And because we are 
about sealing and sig&#173;
ning of deedes, it shal 
not be amisse (brother 
</page>
 
<page sig="87v">
Nicholas) here to 
shewe you, for anti&#173;
quities sake, the ma&#173;
ner of signinge and 
subscribinge of dedes, 
in our auncestours <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
Saxons times, a fa&#173;
shion different from <expan type="y+t">that</expan>
wee vse in these our 
daies, in this that they 
to their deeds subscri&#173;
bed their names (com&#173;
monlye addinge the 
signe of the crose) and 
in the end did sett 
downe a great num&#173;
ber of witnesses, not 
vsinge at that time a&#173;
ny kinde of seale. And 
wee at this day for 
more suerty, both sub&#173;
scribe our names (al&#173;
though that bee not 
verye necessarye as I 
haue aforesayde) put 
to our seales, and 
vse the helpe of testy&#173;
mony besides. 
 
That former fashi&#173;
on contynued through 
out, vntill the time 
of the conquest by the 
</page>
 
<page sig="88r">
Normans, whose ma&#173;
ner by litle &#38; litle at <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
l<expan type="e+_">en</expan>gth preualed am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>gst 
vs, for the first sealed 
Charter in Englande 
is thought to be that 
of kinge Edward the 
confessor to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> abbey of 
<expan type="m+_">Westminster</expan> who (beinge 
brought vp in Nor&#173;
mandy) brought into 
this realme that, and 
<emend err="f">s</emend><expan type="o+_">om</expan>e other of their gui&#173;
ses with him: And af&#173;
ter the comminge of 
Willi<expan type="a+_">am</expan> <expan type="y+e">the</expan> conqueror, <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
Normanz liking their 
owne co<expan type="u+_">un</expan>try customs 
(as naturally all na&#173;
tions do) reiected the 
maner that they fo<expan type="u+_">un</expan>d 
here, and retened their 
owne, as Ingulphus <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
Abbot of Croilande, 
who came in with the 
Conquest witnesseth 
saiynge: The Nor&#173;
mans doe change the 
makinge of writings 
which were woont 
to bee fyrmed in Eng&#173;
lande withe Crosses 
</page>
 
<page sig="88v">
of Golde, and other 
holie signes, into the 
printing waxe, &#38; they 
reiect also the manner 
of the English wry&#173;
ting. Howbeit thys 
was not done all at 
once, but it encreased 
and came forwarde by 
certeine steps and de&#173;
grees, so that first and 
for a season, the kings 
onely, or a fewe other 
of the Nobilitie be&#173;
sides him vsed to seale. 
Then the Noble men 
for the most part, and 
none other, whiche 
thinge a man may see 
in the Historie of 
Battell Abbey, where 
Rycharde Lucy chiefe 
Iustice of Englande, 
in the time of kynge 
Henry the seconde is 
reported to haue bla&#173;
med a meane subiect, 
for that he vsed a pry&#173;
uate seale, when as 
that pertained (as he 
sayde) to the kinge 
and Nobilitie onely. 
</page>
 
<page sig="89r">
At which tyme also 
(as I. Roste noteth it) 
they vsed to ingraue 
in their seales, their 
owne pictures, &#38; co<expan type="u+_">un</expan>&#173;
terfaits, couered with 
a long coate ouer their 
Armours. But after 
this the Gentlemen of 
the better fort tooke vp 
the fashion, and bee&#173;
cause they were not al 
warriours, they made 
seales ingrauen wyth 
their seuerall Coates, 
or shyeldes of armes, 
for difference sake, as 
the same aucthour re&#173;
porteth. At <expan type="y+e">the</expan> length, 
about the time of king 
Edwarde the thirde, 
seales became very co<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
mon, so that not onely 
suche as bore armes 
vsed to seale but other 
men also fashioned to 
them selues signets of 
their owne deuyse, 
some takinge the let&#173;
ters of their owne 
names, some flowers, 
some knottes and flo&#173;
</page>
 
<page sig="89v">
rishes, some birdes, or 
beastes, and some o&#173;
ther thinges, as wee 
now yet daily beholde 
in vse. Some other 
maner of sealinge be&#173;
sides these haue bene 
hearde of amonge vs, 
as namly that of king 
Edward the thirde 
by which hee gaue to 
Norman the hunter, 
the hop and the hop&#173;
towne, with all the 
bo<expan type="u+_">un</expan>des vpside downe, 
and in witnesse that 
it was sooth, hee bitt 
the waxe with hys 
fore tooth. 
 
The like to this 
(Brother Nicholas) 
our reuerend and good 
father, amonge other 
antiquities seruinge 
my purpose, shewed 
me in a lose paper, but 
not very auncientlye 
writen, and therefore 
he willed me to esteme 
of it as I thought 
good, it was as fol&#173;
loweth. 
</page>
 
<page sig="90r">
I Willyan Kinge 
geue to thee Powlen 
Royden, my hop and 
my hoplandes, with 
the boundes vp and 
downe, from heauen 
to earth, from earth 
to hell, for thee and 
thine to dwel, from me 
and mine, to thee and 
thine, for a bow and a 
brode arrow, when I 
come to hunt vppon 
Yarrow. In witnesse 
that this is soothe, 
I bite this waxe with 
mye tooth, in pres<expan type="e+_">en</expan>ce 
of Magg, Maude, 
and Margerye, and 
my thirde sonne Hen&#173;
rye. 
 
Also that of Alberic 
de Veer, conteyning 
the donation of Hat&#173;
field to the which hee 
affixed a short blacke 
hafted knife, like vn&#173;
to an olde halfepenny 
whittle, insteede of a 
seale, with diuers 
such like. But s<expan type="o+_">om</expan>e per 
aduenture wil thinke 
</page>
 
<page sig="90v">
that these were recei&#173;
ued in common vse &#38; 
custome, and that they 
were not rather <expan type="y+e">the</expan> de&#173;
uises &#38; pleasures of a 
few singuler persons, 
such as are no lesse de&#173;
ceiued, then they that 
deme euery charter &#38; 
writinge that hath no 
seale annexed, to be as 
auncient as the Con&#173;
quest, whereas in dede 
sealinge was not co&#173;
monly vsed, til <expan type="y+e">the</expan> time 
of Kinge Edward the 
third, as hath bene al&#173;
ready saide. 
 
The third point is 
deliuerye, which all&#173;
though it be set last, 
is not the least, for af&#173;
ter that a dede be wri&#173;
ten, and sealed, if it be 
not delyuered al the 
rest is to no purpose. 
 
And this deliuerie 
ought to be done by <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
party him selfe, or his 
sufficient warraunt, 
and so it shall bynde 
him whosoeuer wrote 
</page>
 
<page sig="91r">
or sealed the same, and 
by this last acte the 
dede is made perfecte 
accordinge to the in&#173;
tent and effect thereof 
and therefore in deeds 
the delyuerie is to bee 
proued &#38; c. So thus 
you see, that writinge 
and sealinge without 
delyuerie, is nothinge 
to purpose. That sea&#173;
linge, and delyuerie 
where there is no <emend err="wri">wri&#173;</emend>
tinge, worketh no&#173;
thinge, nor writinge 
and delyuery without 
sealinge also maketh 
no dede. Therefore 
they al ought iointlye 
to concurre to make a 
perfect dede, as is be&#173;
fore saide.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="farm(n)">&#182; Farme or ferme.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="farmer(n)">FArme or ferme, is 
the chiefe mesuage 
in a village, or towne, 
and therto beelonging 
great demeasnes of al 
sortes, and hath bene 
</page>
 
<page sig="91v">
vsed to be let for terme 
of lyfe, yeares, or at 
will. 
 
Also the rent that 
is reserued vpon such 
or like leases, is called 
farme, or ferme. 
 
And farmor, or fer&#173;
mour is hee that oc&#173;
cupyeth the farme, or 
ferme, or is lessee 
thereof. 
 
Also in some pla&#173;
ces, and counties eue&#173;
ry lessee, for life, yeres, 
or at will, although 
it be of neuer so smal a 
cottage or house, is 
called farmor, or fer&#173;
mour. 
 
And note that they 
are called farmes, or 
fermes, of the Saxon 
worde <term lang="sx">Feormian</term>, which 
signifieth to feede, or 
yeelde victuall. For 
in the auncient time, 
their reseruat<expan type="o+_">on</expan>s were 
as well (or for the 
more parte) in vic&#173;
tualles, as money, vn&#173;
til at the last, and that 
</page>
 
<page sig="92r">
chiefely in the tyme 
of Kinge Henry the 
first (by agreement) 
the reseruation of vic&#173;
tualles, was tour&#173;
ned into ready mo&#173;
ney, and so hitherto 
hath continued among 
most men.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fee-farm(n)">&#182; Fee farme.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FEe farme is when 
a tenaunt holdeth 
of his lord in fee sim&#173;
ple, payinge to him 
the value of halfe, or 
of the thirde, or of the 
fowerth part or of o&#173;
ther part of the land, 
by the yeare. And hee 
that holdeth by fee 
ferme, ought to doe 
no other thinge then 
is conteyned in the 
feoffement, but one&#173;
ly fealtie, for that be&#173;
longeth to al kinde of 
tenures.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="feoffment(n)">&#182; Feoffement.</form>
</page>
 
<page sig="92v">
<xpln lang="en">FEffement is where 
a man geueth lands 
to an other in fee sim&#173;
ple, and delyuereth 
seisin and possession of 
the lande that is a fe&#173;
offement.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="feoffor(n)" lexeme="feoffee(n)">&#182; Feffor, and Feffee.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FEoffor is he that in&#173;
feffeth, or maketh a 
feoffement to an other 
of landes, or tene&#173;
mentes, in fee simple. 
And feoffee is hee, who 
is infeffed, or to whom 
the feoffement is so 
made.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="firebote(n)">&#182; Fireboote.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FIreboote is neces&#173;
sary wood to burne, 
whiche by the com&#173;
mon lawe, lessee for 
yeres, or for life, may 
take in his grounde, 
although it bee not 
expressed in his lease: 
and although it bee a 
lease by woorde onely 
</page>
 
<page sig="93r">
without writynge: 
But if hee take more 
then is needeful, he shal 
be punished in wast.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fledwite(n)">&#182; Fledwite</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="forestall(v)">&#182; Forstall.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FOrstall, that is to

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="forestaller(n)">&#182; Forstaller.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FOrstaller is he that

tell, or other Mar&#173;
chaundize whatsoe&#173;
uer is salable, by the

to markets, faires, or

he may sel the same a&#173;

and deere price in pre&#173;

The paine for such 
</page>
 
<page sig="94r">
as are conuicte thereof 
&#38;c. is the first tyme, a&#173;

of the thing so bought, 
the seconde time iudg&#173;

The third time impri&#173;
sonment and Raun&#173;
some: The fowerth 
time abiuration of the 
towne &#38;c.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="franchise(n)" lexeme="royal(a)">&#182; Franches Royal.</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="free(a)" lexeme="marriage(n)">&#182; Free mariage.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FRee maryage, is 
when a man seised 
of landes in fee simple, 
gyueth it to an other

(who is the daughter 
Sister or otherwise of 
kinne to the doner) in

</page>
 
<page sig="94v">
of which words they 
haue an estate in spe&#173;
cyall tayle, and shall 
hold the lande of the 
donour quite of al ma&#173;
ner of seruyces vntil 
the fowerth degree be 
past accomptinge th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
selues in the first de&#173;
gree except fealtye, 
which they shall doe 
because it is incy&#173;
dent to all tenures sa&#173;
uinge free almes. And 
such gift may be made 
as wel after mariage 
solempnised as before. 
And a man may geue 
landes to hys sonne 
in free mariage, as 
well as to hys daugh&#173;
ter by the opynyon 
of Master Fitzherbert 
in hys writt of cham&#173;
pertye. H. But it 
appeareth otherwyse 
in Master Lyttleton, 
and in Master Brooke 
<term lang="la">titulo</term> Frankemarry&#173;
age <term lang="la">Placito decimo</term>. And 
so it was holden clere 
in Graies Inne in l<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t 
</page>
 
<page sig="95r">
Anno 1576. 18. Eliz. 
by the right worship&#173;
full master Rhodes th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
reader there.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="freehold(n)">&#182; Freeholde.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="chattel(n)" lexeme="freehold(n)" lexeme="deed(n)" lexeme="law(n)">FReehold is an es&#173;
tate that a mann 
hath in landes or 
tenementes, or profite 
to be taken in fee sim&#173;
ple, taile, for terme of 
his owne life, or for 
terme of an others life 
And vnder that, there 
is no free hold: for he <expan type="y+t">that</expan>
hath estate for yeares 
or holdeth at wil hath 
no free hold: but they 
are called chattels. 
 
And of free holdes 
there are two sortes 
that is to say, freehold 
in deede, and freeholde 
in lawe. 
 
Freeholde in deede, 
is when a mann hath 
entred into landes, or 
tenementes, and is 
seased thereof really, 
actuallye, and in deede 
</page>
 
<page sig="95v">
As if the father seised 
of lands or tenements 
in fee simple dieth, and 
his sonne entreth into 
the same, as heire to 
his father, then he hath 
a freehold in deede by 
his entry. 
 
Freeholde in lawe, 
is when landes or te&#173;
nementes, are dysen&#173;
ded to a man and hee 
may enter into them 
when he wil, but hath 
not yet made his en&#173;
try in deede, as in the 
case aforesaid, if the fa&#173;
ther beinge seysed of 
landes in fee simple die 
seised, and they disc<expan type="e+_">en</expan>d 
to his sonne, but the 
sonne hath not yet en&#173;
tred into th<expan type="e+_">em</expan> in deede, 
nowe beefore his en&#173;
trie hee hath a freehold 
in lawe.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fresh(a)" lexeme="suit(n)">&#182; Freshsuit.</form>
<xpln lang="en">FReshsuit, is when 
a man is robbed, and 
</page>
 
<page sig="96r">
the partye so robbed, 
followeth the felon 
immediatlye, and ta&#173;
keth him wyth the 
manner, or otherwise, 
and then bringeth an 
appeale against him &#38; 
doth conuince him of 
the felony by verdict, 
which thinge beinge 
inquyred of for the 
Queene and found, the 
party robbed shal haue 
restituti<expan type="o+_">on</expan> of his goods 
againe. 
 
Also it maye bee 
sayde that the partye 
made freshesuite al&#173;
though he take not the 
theefe presently, but 
that it be halfe a yere, 
or a yeare after the 
robbery done, before 
hee bee taken: if so bee 
that the partye rob&#173;
bed do what lyeth in 
him, by dylygent in&#173;
quiry and searche to 
take him, yea, and al&#173;
though hee bee taken 
by some other body, 
</page>
 
<page sig="96v">
yet this shal be saide 
freshesuite. 
 
And so freshsuit is 
when the lord com&#173;
meth to distreine for 
rent or seruice, and <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
owner of the beastes 
doth make rescous, &#38; 
driueth them into o&#173;
thers gro<expan type="u+_">un</expan>de that is 
not holden of the lord, 
and the lord followeth 
presently and taketh 
them, this is called 
fresh suit, and so in o&#173;
ther like cases.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gauger(n)" lexeme="deliverance(n)">&#182; Gager of deliuerans.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="demurrer(n)">GAger of deliuerans 
is where one sueth 
a repleuin of goodes 
taken, but he hath not 
delyuery of the goods 
and the other auow&#173;
eth, and the plaintife 
sheweth that the de&#173;
fendant is yet seised 
&#38;c. and praieth that <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
defendant shall gage 
the delyuerance, then 
he shal put in suertye 
or pledges for <expan type="y+e">the</expan> rede&#173;
</page>
 
<page sig="97r">
liuerance, and a wryt 
shall go foorth to the 
Sherife for to redeli&#173;
uer the goodes &#38;c. But 
if a man claime proper 
tie, he shal not gage <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
deliuerance. Also if he 
say that the beastes bee 
dead in the pound, hee 
shal not gage &#38;c. Also 
a man shall neuer gage 
the deliuerance before 
that they be at issue, 
or demurrer in the 
lawe.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ward(n)">&#182; Warde. </form>
<xpln lang="en">WArde, is when an 
inf<expan type="a+_">an</expan>t whose aun&#173;
cestor held by knights 
seruice, is in the ward 
or keeping of the Lord 
of whom those landes 
were holden. And if 
the tenant hold of dy&#173;
uers Lordes dyuers 
landes, the Lord of 
whom the land is hol&#173;
den by prioritie, that 
is to say, by the more 
elder tenure, shal haue 
</page>
 
<page sig="97v">
the wardshippe of the 
infant, but if one te&#173;
nure be as olde as the 
other, then he that first 
happeth to haue the 
warde of the body shal 
keepe it, but in that 
case euery Lord shall 
haue the warde of the 
land that is holden of 
him, but if the tenant 
hold of the Queene in 
chiefe, then she by her 
prerogatiue shal haue 
the ward of the bodie, 
and of al the land that 
is holden of her, &#38; of 
euery other Lord.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="warden(n)">&#182; Wardeine.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="guardian(n)" lexeme="right(n)" lexeme="deed(n)" lexeme="wardship(n)">WArdein most pro<emend err="">&#173;</emend>

<page sig="98r">

And of Wardeines

Gardein in right is

</page>
 
<page sig="98v">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="warning(n)">&#182; Warninge. </form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gavelet(n)">&#182; Gauelate.</form>
<xpln lang="en">GAuelate, is a specy&#173;

</page>
 
<page sig="100r">

will rehaue his tene&#173;
ments, &#38; hold them as 
he did before, let hym 
make agreement wyth 
the Lord, according as 
it is aunciently said. 
 
Hath he not since any 
thing gyuen, nor hath 
he not since any thing 
paied: Then let hym 
pay fyue pounde for 
hys were er before hee 
beecome tenaunt or 
holder againe. 
</page>
 
<page sig="100v">
There bee some co&#173;
pies that haue the first 
Verse thus written. 
 
Nisith yelde, and nisith 
gelde. 
 
And others thus. 
 
Nighesith yelde, and ni&#173;
ghesith gelde. 
 
But these differ not 
in signification, other 
copyes haue it after 
this sort. 
 
Nigondsith felde, and 
nigondsith gelde. 
 
That is to say, let 
him ix. times pay, and 
ix. times repay.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="gavelkind(n)">&#182; Gauelkinde.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="borsholder(n)" lexeme="astre(n)">GAuelkind is a cus&#173;
tome annexed, and 
going with landes in 
Kent called gauelkind 
lands, holden by aun&#173;
cient Socage tenure, 
And is thought by <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
skilfull in Antiqui&#173;
ties, to be called Ga&#173;
uelkind of Gyue al 
kyn, <expan type="y+t">that</expan> is to say, to all 
the kindred in one line 
</page>
 
<page sig="101r">
according as it is vsed 
among the Germans, 
from whom we Eng&#173;
lishmen, and chiefly of 
Kent come. Or els it 
is called Gauelkind of 
gyue all kinde, that is 
to say, to all the male 
children, for kinde in 
Dutch signifyeth a 
male childe. And dy&#173;
uers other like coniec&#173;
tures are made by th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
of the name (Gauel&#173;
kind) which I omyt 
of purpose for short&#173;
nes sake, because that 
here you looke (Bro&#173;
ther Nicholas) as you 
desired me, <expan type="y+t">that</expan> I should 
speak somewhat large 
ly conerninge other 
more needeful matters 
for your purpose, 
which you ar desirous 
to know as touching 
Gauelkind l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ds, both 
because you were born 
in K<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t, &#38; also are most 
abyding there, &#38; there 
fore you thinke to bee 
ignorant of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> maners 
</page>
 
<page sig="101v">
or cust. of your natiue 
c<expan type="o+_">on</expan>try were a foul sh<expan type="a+_">am</expan>e 
 
To satisfie your re&#173;
quest in this: I haue 
therefore set you here 
downe, the auncyent 
customes of Kent, 15 
they haue very truely 
&#38; carefully of late ben 
published, <expan type="w+t">with</expan> some ca&#173;
ses vpon them, gathe&#173;
red out of those bookes 
that make any m<expan type="e+_">en</expan>cion 
hereof, which wyll I 
think cont<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t your de&#173;
sire at full. And first 
you must know, that 
these Gauelkind cus&#173;
tomes are of good an&#173;
tiquitie, brought in 
hither by the Saxons, 
Intes, and Angles 
Germans, from whom 
we Englishmen dys&#173;
cend (as is aforesaid) 
&#38; were by them vsed, 
&#38; left here, &#38; so conti&#173;
nued in force, vntyll 
Willyam Duke of 
Normandie, con&#173;
quered all Englande, 
(Kent onely excepted) 
</page>
 
<page sig="102r">
which he had by com&#173;
posicion &#38; not by con&#173;
quest. And in this c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>&#173;
posycion the valiant 
Kentishmen obtayned 
a graunt of the conty&#173;
nuation of their cus&#173;
tomes of Gauelkinde, 
which euer since they 
haue vsed in the same 
countrey, &#38; thus they 
are as followeth. 
 
&#182; The Customes of Kent 
 
THese are the vsa&#173;
ges &#38; customes, the 
which the comminalty 
of Kent claymeth to 
haue in the tenements 
of Gauelkind, &#38; in the 
men of Gauelkind, al&#173;
lowed in Eire before 
Iohn of Berwicke &#38; 
his Compainions the 
Iustices in Eire in 
Kent, the xxj. yere of 
kind Edw. the sonne 
of king Henrie. 
 
That is to say, that 
all the bodies of Ken&#173;
tishmen be free aswell 
</page>
 
<page sig="102v">
as the other free bo&#173;
dyes of England. 
 
This thing hath ben 
since confessed <emend err="so">to</emend> bee 
true, as it appeareth in 
30. E. I. in Fitzherbert 
titulo Villenage pla&#173;
cito 46. Where it is 
holden sufficient for a 
man to auoyde the ob&#173;
iection of bondage, to 
say that hys father 
was borne in Kent. 
But whether it wyll 
serue in <expan type="y+t">that</expan> case to say, 
<expan type="y+t">that</expan> him selfe was borne 
in Kent, it is (for good 
reason) to be doubted. 
 
2  And <expan type="y+t">that</expan> they ought 
not the Eschetour of 
the king to chose, nor 
euer in any time dyd 
they. But the kinge 
shall take or cause to 
be taken such a one as 
it shall please him, to 
serue him in <expan type="y+t">that</expan> which 
shalbe needefull. 
 
3  And that they may 
their lands &#38; their te&#173;
nements gyue and sel, 
without licence asked 
</page>
 
<page sig="103r">
of their Lordes: Sa&#173;
uing vnto the Lordes 
the rents &#38; the seruy&#173;
ces due out of the same 
tenements. 
 
4  And that all, and 
euery of them, may by 
writ of the king, or by 
plaint, pled for the ob&#173;
tayning of their right, 
aswell of their Lordes 
as of other men. 
 
5  And they claime al<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
so, that the c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>minaltie 
of Gauelkinde menne 
which hold none other 
then tenements of ga&#173;
uelkind nature, ought 
not to come to the co&#173;
mon summons of the 
Eire, but onely by the 
Borsholder, &#38; iiij. men 
of the Borough. Ex&#173;
cept the towns which 
ought to aunswere by 
xij. men in the Eire. 
 
The like to this pri&#173;
uiledge is enioyed at 
this day in the She&#173;
rifs Lathe, where ma&#173;
ny whole borows, be 
excused by the onely 
</page>
 
<page sig="103v">
appearance of a Bor&#173;
sholder, and two, fow-

inhabitants. Borshol&#173;

Saxon wordes <term lang="sx">Bor&#173;
her ealder</term>, that is to

elder of the pledges. 
 
6 And they clayme

in gauelkind bee at&#173;

iudgement of deathe,

&#38; inheritance: And he 
shal hold them by the 
same seruices &#38; cus&#173;
tomes, as his aunces&#173;
tors helde th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>: where&#173;
vp<expan type="o+_">on</expan> it is said in k<expan type="e+_">en</expan>tish 
<term lang="kt">The father to the bough, 
And the son to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> plough.</term>
But this rule holdeth 
</page>
 
<page sig="104r">

haue been holden by 
knightes seruice, doe 
discende. By which

euident, that the ma&#173;

vnderstood al l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ds hol&#173;
den by knights seruice 
to bee of their proper 
nature, discendable af&#173;
ter the common law, &#38; 
that socag. tenure was 
</page>
 
<page sig="120v">
the onely subiect, in 
which thys our cus&#173;
tome of Gauelkinde 
discent, preuayled and 
helde place. 
 
But when mencion 
is here made of So&#173;
cage &#38; knightes fee, it 
must alwayes be vn&#173;
derstanden a Tenure 
long since, &#38; of aunci<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t 
time continued, &#38; not 
now newely or lately

As for example, If

knightes seruice of a&#173;

standing that the te&#173;
nure bee altered, and 
if this bee true in the

the nature together, 
doe gouerne it. And 
</page>
 
<page sig="121v">
therfore, as on the one

So on the other side,

<page sig="122r">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="heriot(n)">&#182; Hariot.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="custom(n)" lexeme="service(n)">HAriot is in two 
sortes, the one ha&#173;
riot custome, the other 
hariot seruice. 
</page>
 
<page sig="130r">
Hariot seruice (some 
say) is alwaies expres&#173;
sed in a mans graunt, 
or dede that he holdeth 
by such seruice to pay 
hariot at the time of 
his death, and this ha&#173;
riot is payable after 
the death of the ten<expan type="a+_">an</expan>t 
in fee simple. 
 
Hariot custome, ys 
where Hariotes haue 
bene payed tyme out 
of mynde by custome. 
And this may be af&#173;
ter the death of te&#173;
naunt for life &#38;c. but 
to speake thereof ge&#173;
nerally. 
 
Hariot is the best 
beast (whether it bee 
Horse, Oxe, or Cow) 
that the tenaunt had 
at the tyme of hys 
death. And may bee 
eyther seysed, or a 
distresse taken for it, 
whether it bee hariot 
seruice, or hariot cus&#173;
tome, to the Lords vse 
of whom the tenaunt 
held, by his Bailife, or 
</page>
 
<page sig="130v">
other officer bel<expan type="o+_">on</expan>ginge 
to his manor. 
 
But of right <expan type="y+e">the</expan> lord, 
nor his officer shoulde 
not take hariot before 
it be presented at the 
next court holden, that 
the tenant is dead, and 
that such a beast is due 
to the Lorde for hys 
hariot.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="haybote(n)" lexeme="hedgebote(n)">&#182; Haybote or hedgebote</form>
<xpln lang="en">HAybote or hedgbot 
is necessarie, stuffe 
to make &#38; mend hed&#173;
ges, which lessee for 
yeres, or for life of c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>&#173;
mon right may take 
vpon <expan type="y+e">the</expan> ground to him 
leased, although it bee 
not expressed in his 
lease, &#38; although it be 
a leas by words with 
out writinge. 
 
Hayebote also may 
bee taken for neces&#173;
sarye stuffe, to make 
Rakes, forkes, and 
suche like Instru&#173;
mentes where with 
</page>
 
<page sig="131r">
men vse in sommer to 
tedde and make haye: 
&#38; so a lessee for yeres 
tooke, and it was al&#173;
lowed hym by his lei&#173;
sor, the rather as I 
suppose, for that such 
instruments are com&#173;
monly made of slender 
vnder wood, which by 
the common lawe the 
lessee for yeares maye 
cutte and take as is 
aforesaid.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hidage(n)">&#182; Hidage.</form>
<xpln lang="en">HIdage, that is to 
bee quit, if <expan type="y+e">the</expan> king 
shal taxe al the lande 
by hides. 
 
Note that a hide of 
land is a whole plow&#173;
lande. And this kind 
of taxinge by hides 
was much vsed in 
olde tyme, as wel for 
prouision of armoure, 
as payementes of mo&#173;
ney, and that chefe&#173;
ly in king Etheldreds 
daies (a kinge in this 
</page>
 
<page sig="131v">
Countrey before the 
conquest) who in the 
yeare of Christ 1006. 
when as the Danes 
landed at Sandwich 
in Kent, taxed al hys 
land by hides thus. 
That euery 310 hides 
of lande should finde 
one ship fornished, and 
euery 8. hides shoulde 
finde one Iacke &#38; one 
sallet, for the defence 
of the realme.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hotchpot(n)">&#182; Hotchpot.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="frank-marriage(n)">HOtchpot, is a med<emend err="">&#173;</emend>

</page>
 
<page sig="132r">

Now if she that is

any part of the xx. a&#173;
cres whereof her fa&#173;
ther died seised: Shee 
must put her landes 
geuen in frankemar&#173;
riage in hotchpot, that 
is to say, she must re&#173;
fuse that gift in fr<expan type="a+_">an</expan>k&#173;
marriage, and suffer 
the land to be c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>mixt 
and mingled together

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hundred(n)">&#182; Hundred.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="cut(v)" lexeme="shire(n)" lexeme="share(n)" lexeme="lathe(n)">HVndredes weare

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="jointure(n)">&#182; Iointure.</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="theft(n)">&#182; Theft.</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="livery(n)" lexeme="seisin(n)">&#182; Liuery of seisin.</form>
<xpln lang="en">LIuery of seisin, is 
a ceremony vsed in 
conueyance of landes 
or tenementes where 
an estate in fee simple, 
fee taile, or a freeholde 
shal passe: and it is a 
testimonial of the wil&#173;
ling departinge from 
al that which he who 
makes the liuery hath 
in the thinge whereof 
liuerie is made: And 
the receyuinge of the 
liuery, is a willing ac&#173;
ceptance by the other 
</page>
 
<page sig="139v">
party, of al that wher 
of the other hath dis&#173;
missed him selfe: And 
was inuented as an 
open and notorious 
thinge, by meanes 
whereof the common 
people might haue 
knowledge of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> pas&#173;
singe or alteration of 
estates from man to 
man, that thereby they 
might be the better 
able to trie in whom 
the right and possessi&#173;
on of lands and tene&#173;
ments were, if they 
should be impanneled 
in Iuries, or other&#173;
wise haue to doe con&#173;
cerning the same. 
 
The common maner 
of deliuery of seisin is 
after this sort done: 
If it be in the open 
feeld where is no buil&#173;
dinge or house, then 
one that can reade ta&#173;
keth the writinge in 
his h<expan type="a+_">an</expan>d, if <expan type="y+e">the</expan> estate shal 
passe by deed, &#38; decla&#173;
reth to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> standers by 
</page>
 
<page sig="140r">
the cause of their mee&#173;
tinge there together 
&#38;c. and then openlye 
readeth the deede in 
English, and after <expan type="y+t">that</expan>
it is sealed, the partye 
who is to depart fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan>
the ground, taketh <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
deed in his handes to&#173;
gether with a clodde 
of the earthe, and a 
twigge or bowe if a&#173;
ny be there, and all 
this he deliuereth to 
the other party in the 
name of possession or 
seisin, accordinge to <expan type="y+e">the</expan>
forme and effect of the 
deed, which before th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
was there reade. But 
if there be a dwelling 
house or building vp&#173;
on the land, then this 
is done there at the 
doore of the same, none 
beeinge lefte at that 
tyme with in the 
House, and the par&#173;
tye deliuereth all the 
aforesaide toogether 
with the ringe of the 
doore in the name 
</page>
 
<page sig="140v">
of seisine or possession, 
and he that receiueth 
the liuerie entreth in 
first alone and shut&#173;
teth too the doore, and 
presently openeth it 
againe, and lettethe 
them in &#38;c. If it be of 
a house whereto is no 
land or ground, the li&#173;
uerie is made and pos&#173;
session taken by the 
deliuerie of the ringe 
of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> doore and deed one<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
ly. And where it is 
without deed either of 
lands, or tenementes, 
there the party decla&#173;
reth by word of mouth 
before wittnesse, the 
estate that he meaneth 
to depart with, and 
th<expan type="e+_">en</expan> deliuereth seisine 
or possession in maner 
as is before said: &#38; so 
the land or tenement 
doth passe as wel wher 
there is no deede as by 
deed, and that by force 
of the lyuerye of sei&#173;
sine: It was agreed 
in Grayes Inne by 
</page>
 
<page sig="141r">
the right worshipfull 
master Snagge, at his 
readinge there in som&#173;
mer an. 2574. that if 
a feoffour deliuer the 
deede in view of the 
land, in name of seisin 
that it is good, because 
that he hath a possessi&#173;
on in him selfe. But 
otherwise it is of an 
atturney, for he must 
goe to the land, and 
take possessi<expan type="o+_">on</expan> him self 
before <expan type="y+t">that</expan> hee can geue 
possession to an other, 
accordinge to <expan type="y+e">the</expan> words 
of his letter &#38;c., And 
where lyuerye of sei&#173;
sin is by viewe, if the 
feoffee doe not enter 
after &#38;c. nothinge 
passeth, for he ought 
to enter indeede.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="letherwite(n)">&#182; Lotherwite.</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mayhem(n)" lexeme="maim(n)">&#182; Mahim, or Maime.</form>
<xpln lang="en">MAhim, is where a&#173;
ny member is hurt 
or taken away, where&#173;
by the partye so hurt 
is made vnperfecte to 
fight: As if a bone 
bee taken out of the 
hedde: or a bone bee 
broken in any other 
part of the bodye: or 
a foote, or hande, or 
finger, or ioynte of a 
foote, or any member be 
cut: or by some wound 
the sinewes be made 
to shrinke, or other 
member, or the fin&#173;
gers made crooked, or 
if an eye be put out, or 
the foreteeth broken, 
or any other thinge 
hurt in a mans bodye 
by meanes whereof 
hee is made the lesse 
able to defende hym 
selfe. 
 
But the cutting off 
</page>
 
<page sig="142r">
of an eare, or nose, or 
breaking of the hinder 
teeth, or such like, is 
no maihim, because it 
is rather a deformity 
of the body, then dimi&#173;
nishinge of strength. 
And if the Iustices 
stande in doubt whe&#173;
ther the hurt be a mai<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
him or not, they vse, &#38; 
wil of their great dis&#173;
cretion take the helpe 
and opinion of some 
skilfull Surgeon, to

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mainprize(n)">&#182; Mainprise.</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="manor(n)">&#182; Mannour.</form>
<xpln lang="en">MAnnour, is a thing 
compounde of dy&#173;
uers thinges, as of a 
house, lande arrable, 
pasture, meadowe,

son, court baron, and

of time, to the contra&#173;
rie whereof mans me&#173;
mory cannot tell, for 
at this day a mannor 
c<expan type="a+_">an</expan>not be made, because 
a court baron cannot 
nowe be made, and a 
manner cannot be <expan type="w+t">with</expan>&#173;
out a court baron, and 
suters, or freeholders,

all the freeholdes ex&#173;
cept one eschete to the 
lord, or if he purchase 
al except one, there his 
manor is gone, for <expan type="y+t">that</expan> it 
c<expan type="a+_">an</expan>not be a manor <expan type="w+t">with</expan>out 
</page>
 
<page sig="143r">
a court Baron (as is 
aforesaid) &#38; a court ba&#173;
ron cannot be holden 
but before sutors, and 
not before one suter, &#38; 
therefore where but 
one freeholde or free&#173;
holder is, there cannot

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="manumission(n)">&#182; Manumission</form>
<xpln lang="en">MAnumission is in 
ij. sorts, the one is 
a manumission expres&#173;
sed, the other a manu&#173;
mission implyed or se&#173;
create. 
 
Manumission ex&#173;
pressed is when <expan type="y+e">the</expan> lord 
maketh a deede to hys 
villein to enfraunches 
him by this word (<term lang="la">Ma&#173;
numittere</term>) which is as 
much to say as to let 
one goe out of an o&#173;
ther mans handes or 
power. 
 
The manner of ma&#173;
numitting or infran&#173;
chisinge in olde time 
moste vsually was 
</page>
 
<page sig="143v">
thus. The Lorde (in 
presence of his neigh&#173;
bors) toke the bonde 
man by the heade say&#173;
ing, I will that thys 
man be free, and there&#173;
with shewed him for&#173;
ward out of his h<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ds, 
&#38; by this he was free 
without any more a 
do. Manumission im&#173;
plyed without thys 
worde (<term lang="la">Manumittere</term>) is 
when the lord maketh 
an obligation to hys 
villein to pay him mo&#173;
ney at a certein day, or 
sueth him where hee 
might enter without 
sute, or graunteth vn&#173;
to his villein an annu&#173;
<emend err="nu"></emend>ity, or lesseth lande 
to hym by deede for 
yeres or for life, and in 
diuers such like cases, 
the villein thereby is 
made free.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="maxim(n)">&#182; Maximes.</form>
<xpln lang="en">MAximes bee the 
foundations of the 
</page>
 
<page sig="144r">
law, and the conclusi&#173;
ons of reason, and are 
causes efficient, &#38; cer&#173;
tein vniuersal propo&#173;
sitions so sure and per&#173;
fect that they may not 
be at any time impea&#173;
ched or impugned, but 
ought alwayes to bee 
obserued and holden 
as strong principalles 
and aucthorities of th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
selues although they 
cannot be proued by 
force of argument or 
demonstrations logi&#173;
cal, but are knowen by 
enduction by the way 
of sence and memory. 
As for example, it is 
a maxime that if a man 
haue issue ij. sonnes 
by dyuers women, 
and the one of them 
purchase landes in fee 
and dyeth wythout 
issue, the other bro&#173;
ther shall neuer be is 
heire &#38;c. 
 
Also it is an other 
maxime that l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ds shal 
disc<expan type="e+_">en</expan>d from the father 
</page>
 
<page sig="144v">
to the sonne, but not 
from the sonne to the 
father for that is an 
ascention &#38;c. &#38; diuers 
such like there be.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mainour(n)">&#182; Maynour.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="hue and cry(n)">MAynour is when

And so we common&#173;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="misprision(n)">&#182; Misprision.</form>
<xpln lang="en">MIsprisi<expan type="o+_">on</expan>, is wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan> on

<page sig="145r">

A Chaplein had fixed

be a Misprision of trea&#173;
son onelye, and no

Also if a man know

but misprision of trea&#173;

so it is in dyuers such 
lyke cases. 
 
And in all cases of 
misprision of Treason 
the party offendor shal 
forfait his goodes for 
euer, and the profites 
of his landes for terme 
of hys life, and hys 
bodie to pryson at the 
Queenes pleasure. 
 
And for Misprision 
</page>
 
<page sig="145v">
of felonie trespasse, 
the offendour shal bee 
committed to pryson 
vntil hee haue founde

his true, which shalbee 
assessed by <expan type="y+e">the</expan> discretion 
of the Iustices before

And note that in e&#173;
<emend err="e"></emend>uery Treason or fe&#173;
lonie is included mis&#173;
prision. &#38; where any 
harm committed trea&#173;
son or felony, the Q.

but of Misprision on&#173;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mortgage(n)">&#182; Mortgage or Morgage.</form>
<xpln lang="en">MOrtgage or Mor&#173;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="mulier(n)">&#182; Mulier.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="intermarry(v)" lexeme="fatherless(a)">MUlier, is a woorde

</page>
 
<page sig="148r">

Verse. 
 
To whom the people 
father is, to him is fa&#173;

To whom the peo&#173;
ple father is, well fa&#173;

And alwayes you

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en">&#182; Negatiua preignans.</form>
<xpln lang="en">NEgatiua pregn<expan type="a+_">an</expan>s 
is when an acty&#173;
on, or information, or 
such like is brought 
against one, and the

barre of the action, or 
otherwise, a negatiue 
plea which is not so 
speciall an aunswere

Affyrmatiue. As for 
example: If hee in 
reuersion enter vppon 
</page>
 
<page sig="149r">
tenant for life suppo&#173;
singe that hee hath a&#173;

ened in fee, this is a

for althoughe it bee 
true, that he hath not 
alyened in fee: yet it 
may be that hee hath 
made an estate in taile 
(which is also a for&#173;
faiture) and then the 
entrie of him in the re&#173;
uersion is lawfull &#38;c. 
Also in a <term lang="la">Quare impe&#173;
dit</term> the Queene makes

in the queens h<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ds by 
the death of W. this is 
</page>
 
<page sig="149v">
a negatiue preignans, 
for it may bee in the 
queens hands other&#173;
wise then by <expan type="y+e">the</expan> deathe 
of W. and it suffiseth 
the queene if it bee in 
her handes by anye 
meanes &#38;c. So it is 
where an Informati<expan type="o+_">on</expan>
was brought in the

bought wool betwene 
shering time &#38; the as&#173;

N. as it is alledged 
&#38;c. this is called a ne&#173;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="naif(n)">&#182; Niefe.</form>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="bare(a)" lexeme="naked(a)" lexeme="contract(n)" lexeme="promise(n)">&#182; Bare, or naked contract.</form>
<xpln lang="en">BAre Contract, or

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="owelty(n)">&#182; Oweltie.</form>
<xpln lang="en">OWeltie, is when 
there is Lord, mes&#173;
ne, and tenant, and the 
tenant holdeth of the 
meine by the same ser&#173;
uices, that the mesne 
holdeth ouer of <expan type="y+e">the</expan> lord 
aboue him. As if the 
ten<expan type="a+_">an</expan>t hold of the mes&#173;
ne by homage, fealtie &#38; 
xx. s. of rent yerely, 
and the mesne hol&#173;
deth ouer of the Lord 
aboue by homage, fe&#173;
altie, and xx shyl&#173;
lynges rent also, this 
is called Owelty of 
seruices.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hearing(n)" lexeme="record(n)" lexeme="deed(n)">&#182; Hearing of Recordes 
and deedes &#38;c.</form>
<xpln lang="en">HEaring of records 
and deedes is, if for 
example, an action of 
det be brought against 
a man vpon an obliga&#173;
tion, &#38; the defendaunt 
appeares to the action, 
&#38; then prayeth that he 
</page>
 
<page sig="153r">
may heare the obliga&#173;
tion where with the 
plaintif chargeth him. 
 
So it is when as 
executors bring an ac&#173;
tion of det, &#38; the def<expan type="e+_">en</expan>&#173;
dant demaundeth to 
heare the testam<expan type="e+_">en</expan>t, vp&#173;
pon this demaunde it 
shalbee reade vnto the 
defendaunt. But if 
it bee in ann other 
terme or after that 
the defendaunt hath 
imparled, then he shal 
not heare it. And so 
as is sayde of deedes, 
is to bee vnderstoode 
of recordes that are 
alleaged against him.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Pape(n)">&#182; Pape.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pope(n)">PApe is an auncyent 
name falsely arro&#173;
gated, or proudly v&#173;
surped by the By&#173;
shop of the onely Ci&#173;
tle of Roome in Ita&#173;
lie, and is common&#173;
ly Englished <expan type="y+e">the</expan> Pope, 
a name truely much 
</page>
 
<page sig="153v">
frequented in our an&#173;
cient yeare books, spe&#173;
cially in the times of 
those kinges, who to 
much ab<expan type="a+_">an</expan>doning their 
imperiall auctoritye, 
and abasinge them 
selues far beneth their 
estate, were not asha&#173;
med to suffer an alyen 
and outlandish Bis&#173;
shoppe, that dwelt a&#173;
boue fiftene hundrede 
miles from them, to be 
soueraigne ouer them 
in theyr owne domy&#173;
nions, and to take fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan>
them not onelye the 
disposition of certeine 
small trifles of none 
accompte, but also the 
nominacion of Arch&#173;
bishoppes, Bishopps, 
Abbots, Deanes, pro&#173;
uosts, appropriations 
of benefices, presenta&#173;
tions to personages, 
vicarages, and gene&#173;
rally of all spirituall 
pers<expan type="o+_">on</expan>s to their prefer&#173;
ments sometimes by 
lapse, and sometymes 
</page>
 
<page sig="154r">
otherwise wherebye 
the kinges princelye 
Prerogatiue was ve&#173;
rye muche abridged 
within their owne 
Realmes.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="partition(n)">&#182; Partition.</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="coparcener(n)" lexeme="parcener(n)">PArtition is a deuy&#173;
dinge of landes dis&#173;
cended by the common 
lawe, or by custome 
amonge coheires or 
parceners wher there 
be ij. at the least whe&#173;
ther they bee sonnes, 
daughters, sisters a<expan type="u+_">un</expan>tz 
or otherwise of kinne 
to the auncestor from 
whom the land disc<expan type="e+_">en</expan>&#173;
ded to them. 
 
And this particy&#173;
on is made iiij. waies 
for the most part, 
whereof three are at 
pleasure and by agree&#173;
ment amonge them, 
the fourth is by com&#173;
pulsion. 
 
One partition by a&#173;
greement is wh<expan type="e+_">en</expan> they 
them selues deuide the 
</page>
 
<page sig="154v">
lande equally into so 
many partes, as there 
bee of them coparce&#173;
ners, and each to chose 
one share or parte, the 
eldest first, and so the 
one after other, as 
they bee of age, except 
that the eldest by con&#173;
sent make the partici&#173;
on, then the choice be&#173;
longeth to the next, &#38; 
so to the eldest last, 
according as it is said 
Who so maketh the 
partition, the other 
must haue the choice. 
 
An other partition 
by agreement is when 
they choose certain of 
their frends to make 
diuision for them. 
 
The thirde partici&#173;
on by agreement is by 
drawing of lots thus: 
first to deuide the land 
into so many parts as 
there bee parceners, 
then to writt euerye 
parte seuerrallye in a 
little scrole, or peece 
of paper or parchment 
</page>
 
<page sig="155r">
and to put the same 
scrolles vp close into 
a hate, capp, or other 
such like thinge, and 
then ech parcener, one 
after an other as they 
bee of age to drawe 
out thereof one peece 
or scrolle wherein is 
written a part of the 
lande which by this 
drawinge is now se&#173;
ueralye alotted vnto 
them in fee simple. 
 
The fowereth par&#173;
tition which is by c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>&#173;
pulsion is when one 
or some of the copar&#173;
ceners would haue 
partition and other 
some will not agree 
thereto, then they that 
so would haue parti&#173;
cion may bring a writ 
<term lang="la">de particione facienda</term> a&#173;
gainst the others that 
would not make par&#173;
tition, by vertu wher 
of they shal be compel&#173;
led to depart &#38;c. Wee 
in Kent (Brother 
Nicholas (where the 
</page>
 
<page sig="155v">
landes are of Gauel&#173;
kinde nature, cal at 
this day our particy&#173;
on Shifting euen the 
very same word that 
the Sax<expan type="o+_">on</expan>s vsed nam&#173;
ly (<term lang="sx">Shiftan</term>) which signi&#173;
fieth to make partici<expan type="o+_">on</expan>
betwene coheires, and 
to assigne to eache of 
them their portion, in 
latyn it is called <term lang="la">Her&#173;
ciscere</term>.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="party(n)">&#182; Parties.</form>
<xpln lang="en">PArties to a fine, or 
deede, are those that 
leuy the same fine, and 
also they to whom the 
fine is leuied: And 
they that make a deede 
of feoffement, and they 
to whom it is made are 
called parties to the 
deede, &#38; so in any other 
like cases.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="patron(n)">&#182; Patron. 
</form>
</page>
 
<page sig="156r">
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="dotation(n)">PAtr<expan type="o+_">on</expan> is he that hath 
a personage, vyca&#173;
rage, free chappell, or 
such like spiritual pro&#173;
motion belonging to 
his mannor, and may 
or ought to giue the 
same benefice when 
and as often as it fal&#173;
leth voyde. And thys 
being patron, or patro&#173;
nage had beginninge 
for the most part by 
one of these iij. waies, 
namely eyther by rea&#173;
son of the foundaty&#173;
on, for that the patron 
or hys auncestours, or 
those from whom hee 
claymes were foun&#173;
ders or buylders of 
the Churche, or by 
reason of Dotation, 
for that they did en&#173;
dowe or geue landes 
to the same for main&#173;
tenaunce thereof, or 
els by reason of the 
grounde because the 
Churche was set or 
builded vp<expan type="o+_">on</expan> their soile 
or grounde: and many 
</page>
 
<page sig="156v">
times by reason of the 
al three.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="perquisite(n)">&#182; Perquisites.</form>
<xpln lang="en">PErquisites are ad&#173;
uauntages and pro&#173;

not yearely: as Es&#173;

Relyefes, wayfes,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="plaintiff(n)">&#182; Plaintife.</form>

</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pleading(n)">&#182; Pleading.</form>
<xpln lang="en">PLeadinge is that 
which c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>meth after 
the count, namly that 
which is contained in 
the barre, replication, 
and reioinder, and not 
that contayned in the 
count it selfe, &#38; there&#173;
fore defaultes in the 
matter of the Count 
are not comprised <emend err="win"><expan type="w+t">with</expan>in</emend>
mispleading, or insuf&#173;
ficient pleadinge, nor 
are remedied by <expan type="y+e">the</expan> sta&#173;
tute of Ieofailes: But 
only that mispleading, 
or insufficyent plea&#173;
ding, committed in the 
barre, replication, and

prouided for.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pound(n)">&#182; Pound.</form>
</page>
 
<page sig="157v">
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pound" lexeme="open(a)" lexeme="closed(a)" lexeme="backside(n)">POundes are in two 
sorts, the one po<expan type="u+_">un</expan>ds 
open, the other po<expan type="u+_">un</expan>ds 
close. 
 
Pounde open, is 
euery place wherein a 
distresse is put, whe&#173;
ther it bee common 
pounde such as are in 
euery towne or lorde&#173;
ship, or whether it bee 
backside, Court, yard, 
pasture, or els what&#173;
soeuer, whether the 
owner of the distresse 
may come to geue th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
meate &#38; drinke with&#173;
out offence for their 
being there, or his c<expan type="o+_">om</expan>&#173;
ming thither. 
 
Pound Close is such 
a place, where the ow&#173;
ner of the distresse may 
not come to geue them 
meat and drinke, with 
out offence, as in a 
close house, or whatso&#173;
euer els place.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="possession(n)">&#182; Possession.</form>
</page>
 
<page sig="158r">
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="actual(a)" lexeme="possession(n)" lexeme="law(n)">POssessi<expan type="o+_">on</expan> is said two 
wayes, eyther actu&#173;
all possession, or posses&#173;
sion in law. 
 
Actuall possession, 
is when a man entreth 
in deede into landes or 
tenements to him dis&#173;
cended, or otherwise. 
Possession in lawe is 
when landes or tene&#173;
ments are discended 
to a man, and he hath 
not as yet really, ac&#173;
tually, and in deede en&#173;
tred into them. And it 
is called possession in 
lawe, because that in 
the eye, and considera&#173;
tion of the lawe, hee 
is deemed to be in pos&#173;
session, for asmuch as 
he is tenaunt to euery 
mans action that will 
sue concernynge the 
same landes, or tene&#173;
ments.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="preamble(n)">&#182; Preamble.</form>
<xpln lang="en">PReamble taketh his

</page>
 
<page sig="158v">
tion (<term lang="la">Pre</term>) before, and

giueth an attaint, <expan type="y+e">the</expan> <expan type="p+_">pre</expan>&#173;
<expan type="a+_">am</expan>ble of which is thus. 
For asmuch as certein

dout very little to giue

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="pretenced(a)" lexeme="right(n)" lexeme="title(n)">&#182; Pretensed right or

<xpln lang="en">PRetensed right or

or tenementes, and an

or sueth for it. Nowe 
</page>
 
<page sig="159v">

whoe so doth sue or

ward come to the pos&#173;
sessi<expan type="o+_">on</expan> of the same l<expan type="a+_">an</expan>ds,
